So this is just like a programming convention. \nJust keep all your passwords everything like that  \n\ninside of av. Another benefit is you usually \ndon't push keys to like an a repository on the  \n\ninternet. Normally you just keep that locally on \nyour computer and then you give that to whatever  \n\nlike the app user or whatever to upload their own \nkeys. Pretty simple stuff. Not rocket science. The  \n\nissue is just nobody actually does this. Nobody \nactually asks their agent to make sure that all  \n\nthis stuff is done. The second is this idea of \nrowle security. So um as you guys are going to  \n\nsee in the next project, we're going to use a \ndatabase and the database that we're going to  \n\nuse is going to called superbase. It's very very \npopular with most vibe coder projects right now.  \n\nAnd basically it's like the single most important \nsecurity feature on planet earth. But um you know  \n\nthese databases out there just don't enable this \nby default because it's just not something that  \n\nyou know is enabled by default. I don't know. \nI'm not a super in-depth computer programmer.  \n\nbut it changes a couple of things and some people \ndon't want to use a database like that. Basically,  \n\nwhat this means is if you don't have this \nfeature enabled called RLS, anybody who has  \n\nthe Superbase URL and then what's called the anon \nkey, which is public by design, can read, write,  \n\nand delete every single row in your database. And \nthat's just how Superbase works by default. And so  \n\nsome very popular vibe coded projects here did not \nhave level security. And basically anybody that  \n\nsigned up for them was given a key, sort of like a \npassword, and then they just use that password to  \n\nsearch up all of the data about every other person \non it. And that led to like a massive massive,  \n\nyou know, issue. Try not to swear in these \nvideos, but it was a cluster. And uh, you know,  \n\nthat ended up with people's ability to delete \nrows and databases, which was stupid. All RLS's.  \n\nBasically says like, hey, user A can only see user \nA's data. User B can only see user B's data. Like,  \n\nobviously, it's not rocket science. Uh, but if you \njust turn this on, you'll fix like 90% of errors.  \n\nOkay, so 83% of exposed superbased databases \ninvolved RLS misconfigurations. uh 83%. That  \n\nmeans like only 17% of exposed superbase issues \nwere not related directly to this one thing that  \n\nI'm telling you about that is basically just a \nbutton. So we're just going to click enable and  \n\nit'll be fine from now on. But just make sure to \nknow RLS is like the main lowhanging fruit. Next  \n\nis something called serverside validation and um a \nbig problem with that is like you know how we just  \n\ndeveloped a website, right? Well, let's say on \ntheir website there was some form and the form was  \n\num I don't know it was like a sign up form and it \nsaid you know enter your email here and then enter  \n\nyour password and then let's say obviously in \norder to implement any sort of sign up mechanism  \n\nwhat we kind of have to do is we have to check to \nsee does the email already exists in our database  \n\nand so what the server side thing what the front \nend thing would do is it would basically say okay  \n\nlet's match the email to something in our database \nwell um when you do this sort of ser uh front-end  \n\nvalidation where you validate like all of the \nlogic on your on your website um it's massive  \n\nsecurity risk because as you just saw a moment \nago, anybody can jump into the code of any website  \n\nand then immediately change it. Like I could \ngo to google.com and then I could, you know,  \n\num hold command shift C or rightclick and then go \ninspect element and then I could select anything I  \n\nwant on the page like this Google search button. \nThen I could actually change it from uh I don't  \n\nknow this input class which is called Google \nsearch and I could change it to nick search.  \n\nAnd now I've just changed Google's website, right? \nAccordingly, what I could also do is if there's  \n\nsome form here when I put my information in that \ndoes my email or whatever, I could actually like  \n\nhave it go through a bunch of email addresses or \na bunch of passwords and then it would change the  \n\nemail and password that is being done the valid \naccordingly. If I had a forum and this forum,  \n\nyou know, was like checking emails or checking \npasswords or something, I could actually go  \n\nthrough and then I could like see the data, see \nthe function that is actually doing the checking  \n\nand then I could like look at it and I could be \nlike, wow. So the logic that that guy is using  \n\nto check if passwords exist is he's checking to \nsee if it's over uh I don't know three characters  \n\nand the maximum password length is 10 characters. \nThat means that uh you know I could theoretically  \n\nenumerate through all passwords between three \nand 10 characters and then I'd be able to find  \n\nout anybody's password. You know it's like this \njust gives attackers way too much information.  \n\nLikewise, it also allows them to do things like, \nyou know, if uh a specific email address already  \n\nexists in the database, what you could do is you \ncould use that same email address, but you could  \n\nchange the request that's being sent. You could \nsign up a million different email addresses using  \n\nsome script. It's just not very scalable, I would \nsay. Um, and it's not very intelligent to do. So,  \n\ninstead, what we do is we actually do server side \nvalidation. And what that means is like basically  \n\nwe have our site right over here. And our site in \ngeneral communicates with some server. And instead  \n\nof doing all the validation over here, okay, \nwhat we do is we say, \"No, what I want to do  \n\nis basically every time somebody tries to create \na new account, I'm not actually going to check  \n\non the site. What I'm going to do is I'm going to \nsend a request over to another backend somewhere,  \n\nsome server. The server is going to be much more \nsecure. And then because it's not on the site,  \n\nnobody can like jump into the server and then \nmake minor changes to, you know, check out the  \n\nlogic and stuff like that. The only thing that \nan attacker would actually ever see is just the  \n\nfact that, oh, the site sent a request to the \nserver. uh but you know the server is like super  \n\npassword gated and you know requires specific \nauthentication. It's not anything that you know  \n\na person could actually use. So that's kind of \neasy. You just ask your AI model, hey you know  \n\nmake sure to server side validate everything. Hey, \nis anything here not being serverside validated?  \n\nOkay, we'll make it serverside validated. And that \ntakes me to number four, which is dependency and  \n\npackage security. Um so when AI generates code, \nsometimes it references packages that don't  \n\nexist as in your AI will literally just make up \na package name. In case you guys didn't know what  \n\npackages are. Basically inside of most folder \nmost coding uh projects nowadays you have like  \n\na node modules folder or something. And these are \nall the services that this um repository is using  \n\nin order to like build my website. And so it's \nusing witch word zod yto q prelude puny code  \n\nreact and so on and so forth. And so as you can \nsee like most websites nowadays are actually like  \n\nvery very heavily based on pre-existing libraries \nthat other people have built which kind of makes  \n\nsense cuz like you know the longer the internet's \naround the more people have built libraries that  \n\nhandle specific features and like obviously you're \ngoing to want to just use somebody else's solution  \n\nrather than build your own wheel and so that's \nwhat a lot of people here are doing. Tailwind  \n\nCSS for instance is a big design library that just \ndoes a lot of the design for you. Arg parse just  \n\nhandles arguments and it's like a simple and easy \nway to you know do bash scripts and functions.  \n\nAnd so the issue is AI just came up with all \nthis stuff, right? But sometimes it'll actually  \n\ndownload or it'll create a name of a module that \ndoesn't actually exist. So for whatever reason,  \n\nI mean the training data or whatever or \njust because of the way statistics works,  \n\nmaybe it'll create a folder here or uh whatever \ncalled acorns and it'll try and download a library  \n\ncalled Acorns and it turns out the library it \nwanted to download is actually called Acorn, but  \n\ninstead it tries downloading Acorns. Now, people \nthat have done a lot of stat stats and stuff like  \n\nthat have like done a lot of this to see, you \nknow, what libraries the um AI models typically  \n\nscrew up on. And so, if it sees like, oh, you \nknow, this always says acorns instead of acorn,  \n\nwhat it'll do is it'll actually these attackers \nwill go through and they'll make a big list of  \n\nall these fake packages. And then they'll actually \ncreate packages on the internet that correspond  \n\nto these. And then these packages will just be \nmalware. So, instead of you downloading Acorn,  \n\nnow you're downloading Acorns. instead of it \nbeing empty and being nothing because it's fake,  \n\nnow you're downloading somebody's package that \nactually has like a string in it that says, \"Hey,  \n\nyou know, when you download this package, I want \nyou to send all of the API keys to this service.\"  \n\nThey as like, \"Oh, yeah, that makes sense. I'm \ndownloading this package. This package is legit.  \n\nLet's do it.\" And then it sends all the API keys \nto the service. So, I mean, like in practice, it's  \n\nnot anywhere near as big of a deal as just like \nserver side validation um issues or RLS, but yeah,  \n\nI mean, like it can be a problem. Another issue \nis AI will typically download like older versions  \n\nand stuff like that if it's not constantly \ninstructed not to which means you might be  \n\npulling in a package with a security vulnerability \nthat you know actually got patched months or years  \n\nago. So in general the recommendation is ensure \nyou get the most updated packages as possible  \n\nas opposed to like you know older ones. And then \nfinally this authentication middleware bit. Um in  \n\ncase you've ever accessed like an authenticated \nservice before typically the way that it works  \n\nis you'll have some thing. So I'm just going to \nopen up a new thing and I'll go cloud.ai. Um,  \n\nI don't know, slashnew. When I click on this, do \nyou notice how it just immediately jump me back  \n\nto sluggin? Claw. The thing is, obviously,  \n\nif you design a website or a web app using HTML, \nCSS, and JavaScript, there's like a million  \n\ndifferent ways you could do so, right? And that's really the  \n\nwhole idea of programming languages in the first \nplace. So in addition to this idea of you know  \n\nlanguages as um standardization you also now have \nthis concept of frameworks as standardization. And  \n\nso frameworks are a level of abstraction that \nare built on top of languages. And what we do  \n\nis we actually just take these three sort of Lego \nblocks and we just construct them in a specific  \n\nway essentially. Okay. You also have um vit right  \n\nnow is super popular and you'll see a lot of apps \ncreated with vit and then on the other side you  \n\nalso have view or really like nux.js and there are \nvarying levels of abstraction here like nux.js is  \n\nactually an it's like a second framework built on \ntop of another framework called uh react which is  \n\nkind of funny. Um nux.js is built on top of view. \nUh you know there are a lot of like dependencies  \n\nand stuff and if you guys remember earlier I said \nwe stand on the shoulders of giants. really do uh  \n\nbecause everything nowadays is built off of \nsomething somebody else built which was built  \n\noff something somebody else built all the way \ndown to where we were programming computers  \n\nwith little ones and zeros on pieces of tape. And \nso in addition to you know our chosen tool and  \n\nthis is the tool I'm going to be using for the \nmost part throughout this course. So excalagger.com/ I \ndon't know settings that's a settings route for  \n\ninstance and so on and so forth and what's really \ncool is because a lot of people have used these  \n\nframeworks before you know like NexJS isn't Neo \nwas developed at least half a decade ago if not  \n\nmore I built tons of projects with it before we \nhad the luxury of agents to do that for us because  \n\nof all this we had a lot of pre-existing projects \nout there on the internet that were built with  \n\nthis and these were all used in the training sets \nof these large language models like you know opus  \n\ngemini and so on and so forth which means make \na long story short If you use a framework, you  \n\nusually get significantly higher quality results. \nNow, frameworks obviously have some baggage as  \n\nwell. The last thing I want to talk \nabout is version control. And to make a long story  \n\nshort, version control just answers the question, \nhow do I store successive updates of files on a  \n\nserver as efficiently as humanly possible? Let me \ngive you a brief little example. I'm sure you know  \n\nback in high school or college or whatever, those \nof us that have gone there in the last few years,  \n\nyou've run into situations where, you know, \nyou're tasked with writing an essay. And then, um, you save it as,  \n\nI don't know, essay docx, let's say. You know, \nit's like a it's like a Google doc or something  \n\nlike that file. And you do it locally in your \ncomputer, maybe Microsoft Word, okay? And then,  \n\num, the next day, you know, you wake up and then \nyou give it to your friend, and your friend gives  \n\nyou some feedback or something like that. Okay? \nSo what they do is they they download the file  \n\nand then they give it back to you and then it's \ncalled essay with feedback.docex. So what you do  \n\nis you take that file but not wanting to lose the \nprevious two files what you do is when you make  \n\nyour updates to it okay you don't save it as essay \nwith feedback you call it essay 2.d dox and then  \n\nthe cycle just repeats itself three or four times \nthen you have essay 3.d dox you have essay 4.x doc  \n\nx and so on and so on and so forth until you have \nessay final superfal important docx. Okay. And  \n\nbefore you know it, you know, your whole downloads \nfolder is a mess. I'm sure we've all probably had  \n\nsituations like this before. Um I know, god \nforbid, I I used to have situations like this  \n\nall the time. Now, that's for writing things like \nessays. I want you to imagine instead of essays,  \n\nwhat you're doing is you're working on an app like \nwe're doing. You'll have app one dot whatever,  \n\napp two dot whatever, app three super important \nfinal whatever. Obviously, these things can get  \n\nout of control really quickly. What compounds \nthe issue is this idea of needing to share files  \n\nbecause this super janky ass method, this could \nwork if it's just you and you understand how you  \n\nname your files and stuff. You could build like a \nnaming convention, right? But what if you want to  \n\nshare this with like 20 other people and 20 people \nall simultaneously need to be able to work on the  \n\nfile that is the most updated without causing \nconflicts and stuff like that and so on and so  \n\nforth. That's really the whole idea behind what \ninitiated version control. And so what we do is  \n\nwe basically have again standing on the shoulders \nof giants um built systems out there that store  \n\nnot only the file itself but store the changes \nbetween files and then just let you access them  \n\nby you know calling a little URL or something and \nthen pulling the file as well as a list of all the  \n\nchanges um that have historically occurred. So \nthat at any point in time, you know, you could  \n\ndownload, let's say, this original file, but \nthen you could jump over here if you wanted to,  \n\njump over here if you wanted to, jump over here \nif you wanted to, jump over here if you wanted to,  \n\nand so on and so forth, all without just needing \nto like pollute your, you know, file system and  \n\nstuff like that. We've obviously set up authentication.  \n\nWe know what like the the general flow looks \nlike. When somebody signs up to your app, they  \n\ncan either get some sort of email notification \nthat they can verify after they're done with  \n\nthat. Then obviously they get they get access. \nA lot of this is handled through authentication  \n\nmiddleware and routes and stuff. Um, but basically \nin our case with payments, we're going to add one  \n\nfinal step. So, basically the user is going to \nstart by signing up, right? So, we already know  \n\nhow to do this. It's pretty straightforward. \nIt's off. After they sign up, they're going  \n\nto be taken to their own private payment page \nwhere they can pick a plan or buy credits. Now,  \n\nif you're doing like a thumbnail lead gener a \nthumbnail generator app, for instance, obviously  \n\nit's probably going to be credit based. Um, if \nyou're doing something that maybe provisions  \n\naccess to like a subscription service or something \nwhere it's like, you know, you pay once and then  \n\nyou do it monthly, then we'll probably do a plan. \num after the payment occurs, they'll get access,  \n\nwhich is referred to as entitlement. They'll \nuse the app, which is called consumption,  \n\nand then in some cases, like if you're using \ncredits, they may run out, in which case they go  \n\nback to the payment. That's sort of how it looks \nunder the hood. In terms of like what's going on,  \n\num we're not going to do the payment processing \nourselves. Basically, everybody on planet Earth  \n\nnow just uses pre-existing options like Stripe. \nAnd the whole idea is when a user clicks subscribe  \n\nor buy credits or whatever um we just have an \nagreement with Stripe where via API we redirect  \n\nthem to a Stripeh hosted checkout page. Stripe \nalready has all the language and handles the card.  \n\nThey do things like advanced fraud detection. \nThey do things like you know handle your tax  \n\nobligations like receipts and stuff like that and \nthen they just send all of that stuff to the user.  \n\nWe never touch a credit card number. We just get \npaid you know in return for doing this. Stripe or  \n\nthese other payment processors they usually make \nlike a couple percentage points. I think Stripe  \n\nright now is 2.9 plus 30 cents per transaction \nor something. Um, I'm very happy to pay the  \n\n2.9 cents not to have to deal with this because \nin addition to this being like a real cluster,  \n\nyou know, don't want to swear too much on this, \nbut in addition to being a real pain in the ass,  \n\nuh, it is also kind of like a liability legally. \nAnd, you know, as app developers, I want to  \n\nfocus on building the app and marketing it and \nstuff. That's what 99% of my work needs to be,  \n\nnot handling the legalities. So when the payment \nsucceeds, and it will if you know the the card  \n\nis not fraudulent, if they cross their tees and \ndot their eyes, Stripe will send our backend a  \n\nweb hook, which is basically just like an event \nnotification that says, \"Hey, this user paid,  \n\nthis user that you've already signed up for.\" \nSo we have all their information and so so on  \n\nand so forth. Their ID um is good to go. Then your \nbackend will receive that. It'll update a field on  \n\nthe user's record in your database. So in our case \nour superbase that'll be something like plan is  \n\nnow pro or credits are 50 and then your app knows \nwhat it's allowed to do and then you know as it  \n\nuses and consumes those credits every single time \nit'll basically make a request to the database  \n\njust reducing that a little. Um and then you know \nbecause it's like this you can now do a variety of  \n\nof cool features. You could provision additional \napp um credits or usage if you want. You have the  \n\nability to like upgrade people, downgrade \npeople, moderate your app and everything.  \n\nSo, three models are onetime purchase, which \nis basically where you pay once, you get  \n\naccess forever. It's really easy to do. You're \nbasically just selling a service, right? So,  \n\nyou just flip a boolean on their account. Um, they \nsign up via the Superbase O function. Now, they  \n\nhave like a little like user table. Inside the \nuser table, there's a field that says subscribe,  \n\nquestion mark, and then it's either true or false. \nIn subscription, it's pay monthly and then the  \n\naccess continues. Stripe has a bunch of features \ninside of it that manage recurring subscriptions  \n\nor billing um on let's say a monthly or a weekly \nor an annual basis. And basically what happens  \n\nis once you have the payment success or payment \nfailure um you'll receive a web hook and then  \n\nyou just toggle that access and then Stripe will \nmanage that for you every single month. Basically  \n\nuh whenever a a successful payment comes in, \nyou'll keep it. If a payment failure comes in,  \n\nthen you'll flip it. And then finally that \ncredit based usage where you basically just  \n\nbuy a bucket of different um tokens or credits or \nwhatever the heck. every time they hit your API  \n\nto generate a thumbnail or scrape your leads, \nyou decrement the balance. When it hits zero,  \n\nyou will send them back to checkout and then \nforce them to do it again. So, all of the apps  \n\nthat we built in this course can use any of \nthese. So back \nin the day, right, 30, 40 years ago, if you were  \n\na cool startup and you were working on some new \ncomputer system, okay, and you were smarter than,  \n\nyou know, your competitors and you spent more time \non it than competitors, you might have had like  \n\na small edge and maybe with that small edge, you \nwould have carved out like an operating system or  \n\nsomething like that. And maybe potentially you \nwould have called that Windows OS and then you  \n\nwould have sold that at scale to people and that's \nhow you would have made your money. So if you  \n\nthink about it like Microsoft and Windows, Windows \nuh uh was like software mode, software as a mode.  \n\nSame thing with like the Apple brand of computers. \nSame thing with like I don't know applications  \n\nafterwards like Photoshop and like various \ndatabases and stuff like that. Businesses that  \n\nwere built entirely off of software made sense \nlike 30 or 40 years ago because software back then  \n\nwas very difficult to make. Okay. Well, nowadays \nsoftware is very easy to make as hopefully I've  \n\njust demonstrated here over the course of the \nlast six or seven hours or however long this  \n\ndamn course ended up being. So, we've transitioned \npretty heavily. Unfortunately, a lot of people are  \n\nstill stuck in that old school world where they \nthink, okay, that they're going to put together  \n\nthis incredible software solution that nobody else \nin the market can do because they're smart and  \n\nthey're intelligent and they're they're visionary, \nthey're a forward thinker. The probability that  \n\nyou do that is extremely low. It's extremely low. \nNot just because, you know, nobody's as special as  \n\nwe think we are, but uh because there are soon to \nbe 100 million times as many software engineers in  \n\nthe form of AI agents than there ever would have \nbeen human beings. And so moes are no longer made  \n\nthrough software. Your edge in the market is no \nlonger in the cool algorithm that you come up with  \n\nor whatever the heck. Okay. where the moat is is \nin distribution, which is fundamentally different.  \n\nAnd rather than play by the software mode playbook \nlike everybody in their mom is doing right now,  \n\num I encourage you to play by the distribution \nmode playbook. Hypothetically, why do you  \n\nthink Netflix is still Netflix in a world where \nliterally anybody could just tell their agent,  \n\nhey, here's Netflix.com. can you go and recreate \neverything about it and then I don't know you  \n\nknow host the the servers and set up databases \nand set up all the infrastructure and then like  \n\nhave a little login page everything like that the \nreason why Netflix is not broke you know today is  \n\nbecause Netflix contains within it distribution \nit contains a collection of clients vendors  \n\nrelationships their staff their team their culture \nand so on and so on and so forth and these are  \n\nthings that an AI agent and until they, you know, \nhook it up to the cloning vats and start pumping  \n\nout clients, vendors and and staff members, um, \ncannot currently do or reproduce. And so software,  \n\nwhich used to be like the golden goose, right, \nhas basically been replaced almost completely  \n\nwith distribution. Okay? And in a world where \nanybody could build any software, the only thing  \n\nthat makes your software different from somebody \nelse's software are the customers essentially the  \n\nvendors, the relationships, and the staff on \nyour team um that manage it. So, highle advice aside, \nhow do you actually do the big thing that I  \n\nthink a lot of people are wondering? How do you \nprice your product? Well, what I want to do next  \n\nis talk about essentially my core principle for \npricing. And this is a core principle that I've  \n\nused to generate tens of millions of dollars in \nrevenue across a variety of businesses, small  \n\nto mid-size businesses, my own agencies, digital \nmarketing businesses, uh a couple multi-billion  \n\ndollar portfolio companies. And um this is \nsomething that I just I I continuously return  \n\nto you because it's the easiest and simplest \nway to price. So, it's called VBP or valuebased\n\npricing. And so in value based pricing, what \nyou do is you price based off the value that  \n\nyour app delivers, not on your costs or your \ncompetitor's pricing. You know, and I think a  \n\nlot of people are like, well, I have to anchor to \nmy competitor's price, don't I? And to me, I say  \n\nI mean like obviously your competitor's price is a \nfactor and people are going to be doing some price  \n\nshopping. But if you focus on the value instead of \nat what your competitors price rather than running  \n\nuh down to the very bottom and it being like a \nrace down below like a lot of these freelance  \n\nplatforms and stuff where somebody bids nine, \nanother bids eight, another bid seven till you're  \n\nat freaking zero. What we do is we just establish \nhow tremendously valuable a product is and then  \n\ncharge such a vanishingly small percentage of said \nvalue that the customers are bought in regardless.  \n\nI mean to them it's like a it's it's a pittance. \nAnd I know this because I also purchase software.  \n\num what ultimately a lot of B2B software users \nare looking for is something that maximizes their  \n\nreturn on investment and minimizes the friction \ninvolved in making the purchase decision. So let  \n\nme explain what I mean by all this. There are two \ntypes of value that you can produce at least in a  \n\nB2B environment. In a B2C environment you have \nadditional types of value. Those types of value  \n\nare nonmaterial and so it will not be discussed. \nYou have the ability to directly reduce their  \n\nexpenses which is the money that your product \nwill save a customer if they use you. And then  \n\nyou also have the ability to significantly \nincrease their revenue in opportunity cost,  \n\nwhich is also termed the additional money \ncustomers can now earn because they use your  \n\nproduct. I think direct expense savings are pretty \nsimple, right? If I have product X and product X  \n\ncurrently costs the user $100 a month and I say, \nhey, I just built product Y. Product Y only costs  \n\n$5 a month. What do we have there? What I've done \nis, if you think about it, I've saved the customer  \n\n$95 every month. And so the direct expense savings \nare 95 bucks. Easy. Now, an opportunity cost. Now,  \n\nopportunity cost is a little bit different. \nInstead of focusing specifically on what the  \n\ndelta between, you know, their old thing and then \ntheir new thing is, what we focus on instead is  \n\nhow much time do they save and what is the \ncost of that time. So I'll give you a brief  \n\nlittle example. Let's say hypothetically you \nare currently a salesperson. You work at big  \n\ncompany. Big company pays you on average about \n$100 per hour. Right now, as part of your day,  \n\nyou do some [ __ ] admin task. That admin task \ninvolves filling out some CRM or whatever. And  \n\non average, every single day, you have to spend \n2 hours doing this. Okay? So, they're paying you  \n\n$100 an hour. You're doing an admin task that is \nworth 2 hours. If you hypothetically could build a  \n\nsoftware solution that enable this salesperson \nto spend zero minutes doing this admin task,  \n\nhow much money are you saving this salesperson \nright off the bat? You're saving this company  \n\nright off the bat. You're saving $2* $100 an hour \nor $200 an hour. That is your direct expense. But  \n\nif you think about it, what do sales people do? \nSalespeople are tasked with increasing the revenue  \n\nof the business that they work in. Hypothetically, \nlet's say if instead of focusing on admin time,  \n\nthey then focus on sales, aka calling people or \nwhatever. in those two hours they could contact  \n\n50 people and if in one in 50 people end up \npurchasing a small product or something like  \n\nthat okay which is their conversion rate and if \nthe average cost of that product is let's say  \n\nuh I don't know $500 then by saving 2 hours a day \nnot only are you adding $200 sorry $200 an hour  \n\nI meant $200 not only you adding $200 back to the \ncore business that you built the software platform  \n\nfor you're also adding If you think about it, \nan additional $500 in revenue. So this is minus  \n\n$200 of expense. Okay? And this is plus $500 \nin revenue. And if you think about the total  \n\namount now that this software product is saving \nor generating the business, we have come to  \n\n$700 in value and that's per day. Okay. So the \nexpense portion is about saving money whereas  \n\nthe revenue portion is about reallocating the time \nthat your software solution is now saved to allow  \n\na person at a business to focus on higher revenue \nthings that actually produce for a company. And in  \n\npractice usually these expenses are much smaller \nthan the total amount of opportunity cost aka  \n\nthe revenue that you are unlocking. So you know \nhow earlier we built a thumbnail generator? Well  \n\nhere's a quick example of what that might look \nlike. You have direct cost savings. Let's say  \n\nright now whatever system that they are using \ncost them $50 a thumbnail and they generate 20  \n\nthumbnails a month. That means that by switching \nover to nyx thumbnail company.co, also known as  \n\nthumbly, uh you'll save $1,000 a month. But when \nyou take into account the time savings revenue,  \n\nokay, you move this 2 hours per thumbnail design \nperiod to 5 minutes. What you're doing is you're  \n\nsaving 115 minutes. And hypothetically, let's \nsay these 115 minutes times 20 are 2,300 minutes  \n\na month. Let's say with this additional time, \nwhich works out to be approximately 40 hours,  \n\nthis designer or something can actually make \nan additional $1,000 a month as well. Well,  \n\nnow the total value created is $2,000 a month. \nWhat we're doing as somebody that is developing  \n\na thumbnail product is we're pricing at a very \nsmall percentage of the total value we produce,  \n\nso maybe 10%. So, we charge 200 bucks a month. And \nthen the customer ends up making 10 times as much  \n\nmoney as they spend with us for using our service. \nNow, obviously, this is going to be an average,  \n\nright? Every customer is going to be different. \nSome people are going to produce way more value  \n\nthan your $2,000 a month estimate. Others are \ngoing to produce less value. But because you  \n\ncan't price individually per every new customer \nthat you onboard, you have to pick a specific  \n\npackage price. What we've done now is we've \nbasically used facts and logic to work out what  \n\na strong value would be for a core product and \nthen how much to charge for it instead of relying  \n\non things like competitor analysis or worrying \nabout what the dude in the next cubicle over is  \n\ndoing. And so this is a much more sustainable and \nhigher ROI way of pricing. You're pricing based  \n\noff value. You're not pricing based off of what \neverybody else is doing. This young viewers is  \n\nhow you zigg when everybody else zags. Once you've \nestablished some sort of price for your product,  \n\nmy recommendation is I actually establish \nthis as my top price. So, for instance,  \n\num let's say I kind of screwed up the uh writing \nhere, but let's say this is like your top tier,  \n\nthis is your mid tier, and this is your entry \ntier. You know how there's typically multiple  \n\nsubscription tiers for a product, right? Assuming \nyou're doing a monthly thing. What you do is you  \n\ntake the top and then you set that one at uh you \nknow, let's say 200 bucks a month. And what you  \n\ndo is you set your middle at approximately half of \nthat and you set that at about 100 bucks a month.  \n\nbut you uh reduce the ROI to compensate. So in \nthis case, you know, previously I'm offering a  \n\n10x ROI on my top. Well, now I'm offering an 8x \nROI on my mid and then a 6x ROI on my entry. And  \n\nso in this way, you have like a reasonable product \ndifferentiation. You have a reasonable spread of  \n\nvalues. And this is enough to get you started \nbefore, you know, you can get enough customers,  \n\nyou can actually run like strong analytics to \ndetermine whether or not people would actually  \n\nprefer a $37 a month product or a $58 a month \nproduct or whatever. And that's honestly all there  \n\nis to it. To make a long story short, what I do \nanytime I'm pricing an app, uh, Pel, an automation  \n\nservice, a back-end tool, even like a big business \ndeal, back when I was um, operating a content  \n\nwriting agency and we were trying to work out how \nmuch to price our product, uh, we do it all based  \n\noff of value pricing, which is determining how \nmuch value our product has to the user to begin  \n\nwith, an expense, an opportunity cost, and then \njust charging a small percentage of that. And  \n\nthat usually ends up being somewhere between 10 to \n20% of the total ROI. And as a business owner, I'm  \n\nthinking like, hey, I could put my money in the \nS&P 500, I could make 8% a year, or I could give  \n\nmy money to NYX Co. and then I'd make a,000% on my \ninitial investment. What am I going to do with my  \n\nmoney when presented that choice? Obviously, I'm \ngoing to give it to Nyx Co. right now. But I'll leave that for another day.  \n\nHopefully, at least now you guys understand how to \nprice set products. But that takes us to the next  \n\nbig thing, okay? which is well there's pricing the \nproduct but how do I actually get my product out  \n\nthere okay how do I actually market this amazing \nthing that I have developed well there are three  \n\nmajor ways the first is outbound a lot of people \nshy away from outbound but outbound was the very  \n\nfirst thing that I did when I started my first \nbusiness my first real business about 10 years ago  \n\nI went door to door literally knocking on business \ndoors coming in trying to shake their hands and  \n\nthen sell them on my little marketing package it's \nvery difficult but this is ultimately what works  \n\nSo in this case, you go directly to people who \nneed your product. You can consider this like a  \n\nsniper rifle approach. The two most popular \nmethods right now are cold email, which is  \n\nwhere you actually craft an email, usually using a \ntemplate that appears to be personalized onetoone  \n\ncommunication between you and the person that's \nreading. And so in this way you're reaching  \n\nout directly to them. This is you. This is them \nand this is you bridging that gap. That's why it's  \n\ncalled outbound. The other main way to do this \nis through cold DMs. And typically you do this  \n\non B2B platforms like LinkedIn. Cold DMs stands \nfor direct messages. It's just the same thing  \n\nas cold email. It's just instead of using like an \nemail inbox, which tends to be the the highway to  \n\nthe soul, um we're instead using like a LinkedIn \nmessage box or like an Instagram message box,  \n\nbut it's the exact same idea. Some caveats here. \nThis works really well for B2B products, but it is  \n\npretty difficult for consumer products, aka B2C \nproducts, since most of the lead sources where  \n\nyou're going to scrape this information do tend to \nbe B2B. Like for instance, if you think about it,  \n\nLinkedIn is entirely a commercial platform. If \nyou sign up to LinkedIn, odds are you're signing  \n\nup for employment or business purposes. So, you \ncan feel pretty safe that most of the people that  \n\nyou're scraping on LinkedIn, you're doing so \nbecause it is a B2B sort of transaction. Hey,  \n\nI can help you at XC Corp. So, for the most part, anytime I sell B2B, \nI start with Outbound. The other big benefit to  \n\noutbound is it's just really fast. It is way \nfaster than any of the other methods on the  \n\nlist. It's also way harder, right? you sort of \nhave to put your freaking ego on the line. That said,  \n\nthis is not the only thing that you can do. It is \nnot the only way. There are a variety of others.  \n\nThe second is inbound, which I think a lot more \npeople here are probably familiar with. Inbound,  \n\nif you kind of think about it logically, is \nkind of like what I'm doing right now. What  \n\nI'm doing is I'm creating very valuable content. \nI'm building a very strong and large audience of  \n\npeople that enjoy consuming my content. The issue with  \n\ninbound, despite the fact that, you know, if I'm \nhere and then they're here, despite the fact that  \n\nthey're now coming to you, is it's a lot slower. \nIt takes a lot longer to build. Okay. And what a  \n\nlot of people don't realize is they'll watch some \nvibecoded course or whatever and then be like,  \n\nI'm going to make my own app business. It's going \nto be awesome. And then you're like,  \n\n\"Okay, I have a megaphone. This is awesome.\" And \nthen what you don't realize is every other person  \n\nalso has a megaphone. And then the big people that \nare pretty established have megaphones that are  \n\nway bigger than you. And so \ngrowth on inbound is extraordinarily difficult  \n\nas a beginner unless you have some pre-established \nsuccess or pre-established wins. And most people  \n\nthat are starting these sorts of things that \nimmediately turn to I'm going to post one thread  \n\non Instagram every day for the next year. Uh \nthey they don't understand this. And so what  \n\nends up happening with a lot of inbound is because \nit takes so much longer to build and people are so  \n\nunprepared for this, they end up just screeching \ninto the void, never achieving anything. and then  \n\nquitting before they actually build up the amount \nof momentum required to succeed. If I could graph  \n\nsort of like the differences between inbound \nand outbound. And I love graphing stuff. Um, if you use inbound,  \n\nokay, what happens is you start at this time step \none and then you make no money for a long time  \n\nand then all of a sudden your brand picks up and \nthen you make a ton of money. This \nover here might be one year. And so with inbound,  \n\nwhat we've done is we've sacrificed all our \nmoney in the first year for money after year  \n\nnumber one. And with outbound, what we've done \nis we've sacrificed money after year 1 for money  \n\nbefore year 1. Um in reality things are never \nfully black or white and so the recommendation  \n\nis usually to do some sort of blended take of both \nwhere you know at the beginning you start mostly  \n\nwith outbound. Okay. And then as time goes on you \nshift more to inbound which allows you to generate  \n\nrevenue quickly without also tapering out. The third major way that you \ncould market as somebody that develops and builds  \n\nproducts is instead of trying to build your own \ndistribution via you know outbound like we talked  \n\nabout earlier or inbound where people come to you. \nWhat you do is you just take somebody else okay  \n\nsomebody like Nick Sarif and you say yo Nick can \nyou market my product for me and then I'll just  \n\npay you 30%. So it's more predictable for you. \nMaybe it won't necessarily be cheaper, but it's  \n\nprobably cheaper than you initially buying a bunch \nof software platforms, getting a bunch of cold  \n\nemails, uh getting a a beautiful studio and all \nthat stuff. So, the whole idea is you just partner  \n\nwith someone who has pre-established distribution \nand then you make it a win-win for them. You're  \n\nlike, \"Hey, man.
